<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Number</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Zero" and "Naught" redirect here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/0_(disambiguation)" title="0 (disambiguation)">0 (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Zero_(disambiguation)" title="Zero (disambiguation)">Zero (disambiguation)</a>. For the music album, see <a href="/wiki/Naught_(album)" title="Naught (album)">Naught (album)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the number and digit 0. Not to be confused with the letter <a href="/wiki/O" title="O">O</a> or the <a href="/wiki/O_mark" title="O mark">O mark</a> used to represent affirmation.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable plainlinks selfreference noprint selfref" role="note">For <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(technical_restrictions)#Forbidden_characters" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (technical restrictions)">technical reasons</a>, "0#" redirects here. For the concept in set theory, see <a href="/wiki/Zero_sharp" title="Zero sharp">Zero sharp</a>.</div>

numerals">Bangla</a></th><td class="infobox-data">০</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>0</b> (<b>zero</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Number" title="Number">number</a> representing an empty <a href="/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantity</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Place-value_notation" title="Place-value notation">place-value notation</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a>, 0 also serves as a placeholder <a href="/wiki/Numerical_digit" title="Numerical digit">numerical digit</a>, which works by <a href="/wiki/Multiplication" title="Multiplication">multiplying</a> digits to the left of 0 by the <a href="/wiki/Radix" title="Radix">radix</a>, usually by <a href="/wiki/10" title="10">10</a>. As a number, 0 fulfills a central role in <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Additive_identity" title="Additive identity">additive identity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_structure" title="Algebraic structure">algebraic structures</a>.
</p><p>Common <a href="/wiki/Names_for_the_number_0_in_English" title="Names for the number 0 in English">names for the number 0 in English</a> are <i>zero</i>, <i>nought</i>, <i>naught</i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>), <i>nil</i>. In contexts where at least one adjacent digit distinguishes it from the <a href="/wiki/O" title="O">letter O</a>, the number is explicitly pronounced as <i>oh</i> or <i>o</i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>). Informal or <a href="/wiki/Slang" title="Slang">slang</a> terms for 0 include <i>zilch</i> and <i>zip</i>. Historically, <i>ought</i>, <i>aught</i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>), and <i>cipher</i>, have also been used.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Names_for_the_number_0" title="Names for the number 0">Names for the number 0</a> and <a href="/wiki/Names_for_the_number_0_in_English" title="Names for the number 0 in English">Names for the number 0 in English</a></div>
<p>The word <i>zero</i> came into the English language via French <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">zéro</i></span> from the <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">zero</i></span>, a contraction of the Venetian <span title="Venetian-language text"><i lang="vec">zevero</i></span> form of Italian <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">zefiro</i></span> via <i>ṣafira</i> or <i>ṣifr</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In pre-Islamic time the word <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṣifr</i></span> (Arabic <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">صفر</span></span>) had the meaning "empty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithsonian_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonian-2">[2]</a></sup> <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Sifr</i></span> evolved to mean zero when it was used to translate <span title="Sanskrit-language romanization"><i lang="sa-Latn">śūnya</i></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">शून्य</span>) from India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithsonian_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonian-2">[2]</a></sup> The first known English use of <i>zero</i> was in 1598.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Italian mathematician <a href="/wiki/Fibonacci" title="Fibonacci">Fibonacci</a> (c. 1170–1250), who grew up in North Africa and is credited with introducing the decimal system to Europe, used the term <i>zephyrum</i>. This became <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">zefiro</i></span> in Italian, and was then contracted to <span title="Venetian-language text"><i lang="vec">zero</i></span> in Venetian. The Italian word <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/zefiro" title="wikt:zefiro">zefiro</a></i></span> was already in existence (meaning "west wind" from Latin and Greek <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Anemoi" title="Anemoi">zephyrus</a></i></span>) and may have influenced the spelling when transcribing Arabic <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṣifr</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifrah_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ifrah-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_usage">Modern usage</span></h3>
<p>Depending on the context, there may be different words used for the number zero, or the concept of zero. For the simple notion of lacking, the words "nothing" and "none" are often used. Sometimes, the word "nought" or "naught" is used.
</p><p>It is often called "oh" in the context of reading out a string of digits, such as <a href="/wiki/Telephone_number" title="Telephone number">telephone numbers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Street_address" title="Street address">street addresses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Credit_card_number" title="Credit card number">credit card numbers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_time" title="Military time">military time</a>, or years (e.g. a phone number 555-0123 would be pronounced "five five five oh one two three"; a year such as 1907 is often pronounced "nineteen oh seven"). The presence of other digits, indicating that the string contains only numbers, avoids confusion with the letter O. For this reason, systems that include strings with both letters and numbers (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_postal_code" title="Canadian postal code">Canadian postal codes</a>) may exclude the use of the letter O.
</p><p>Slang words for zero include "zip", "zilch", "nada", and "scratch".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thesaurus_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-thesaurus-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Nil" is used for many sports in <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a>. Several sports have specific words for a score of zero, such as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Love_(tennis)" title="Love (tennis)">love</a>" in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lawn_tennis" title="Lawn tennis">tennis</a> – from French <i>l'oeuf</i>, "the egg" – and "<a href="/wiki/Duck_(cricket)" title="Duck (cricket)">duck</a>" in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cricket_(sport)" title="Cricket (sport)">cricket</a>, a shortening of "duck's egg"; "goose egg" is another general slang term used for zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thesaurus_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-thesaurus-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Near_East">Ancient Near East</span></h3>
<table align="right" cellpadding="8" cellspacing="0" style="float:right; clear:right; text-align:center; border: 1px solid">
<tbody><tr>
<th>nfr<br/> 
</th>
<td>heart with <a href="/wiki/Trachea" title="Trachea">trachea</a><br/>beautiful, pleasant, good
</td>
<td><table class="mw-hiero-table mw-hiero-outer" dir="ltr"><tbody><tr><td> <table class="mw-hiero-table"><tbody><tr> <td><img alt="F35" height="38" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_F35.png?9f378" style="margin: 1px;" title="F35"/></td></tr></tbody></table> </td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_numerals" title="Egyptian numerals">Egyptian numerals</a> were of <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">base 10</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> They used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hieroglyphs" title="Hieroglyphs">hieroglyphs</a> for the digits and were not <a href="/wiki/Positional_notation" title="Positional notation">positional</a>. By 1770 BC, the Egyptians had a symbol for zero in accounting texts. The symbol <a href="/wiki/Nefer" title="Nefer">nfr</a>, meaning beautiful, was also used to indicate the base level in drawings of tombs and pyramids, and distances were measured relative to the base line as being above or below this line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the middle of the <a href="/wiki/2nd_millennium_BC" title="2nd millennium BC">2nd millennium BC</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_mathematics" title="Babylonian mathematics">Babylonian mathematics</a> had a sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Sexagesimal" title="Sexagesimal">base 60</a> positional numeral system. The lack of a positional value (or zero) was indicated by a <i>space</i> between sexagesimal numerals. In a tablet unearthed at <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a> (dating to as early as 700 BC), the scribe Bêl-bân-aplu used three hooks as a <a href="/wiki/Free_variables_and_bound_variables" title="Free variables and bound variables">placeholder</a> in the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_numerals" title="Babylonian numerals">Babylonian system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiref1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-multiref1-8">[8]</a></sup> By 300 BC, a punctuation symbol (two slanted wedges) was co-opted to serve as this placeholder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Babylonian placeholder was not a true zero because it was not used alone, nor was it used at the end of a number. Thus numbers like 2 and 120 (2×60), 3 and 180 (3×60), 4 and 240 (4×60) looked the same, because the larger numbers lacked a final sexagesimal placeholder. Only context could differentiate them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Columbian_Americas">Pre-Columbian Americas</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cero_maya.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="150" data-file-width="303" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Cero_maya.svg/220px-Cero_maya.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Cero_maya.svg/330px-Cero_maya.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Cero_maya.svg/440px-Cero_maya.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cero_maya.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maya numeral zero</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar" title="Mesoamerican Long Count calendar">Mesoamerican Long Count calendar</a> developed in south-central Mexico and Central America required the use of zero as a placeholder within its <a href="/wiki/Vigesimal" title="Vigesimal">vigesimal</a> (base-20) positional numeral system. Many different glyphs, including the partial <a href="/wiki/Quatrefoil" title="Quatrefoil">quatrefoil</a> were used as a zero symbol for these Long Count dates, the earliest of which (on Stela 2 at Chiapa de Corzo, <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a>) has a date of 36 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the eight earliest Long Count dates appear outside the Maya homeland,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> it is generally believed that the use of zero in the Americas predated the Maya and was possibly the invention of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmecs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Many of the earliest Long Count dates were found within the Olmec heartland, although the Olmec civilization ended by the <span class="nowrap">4th century BC</span>, several centuries before the earliest known Long Count dates.
</p><p>Although zero became an integral part of <a href="/wiki/Maya_numerals" title="Maya numerals">Maya numerals</a>, with a different, empty <a href="/wiki/Tortoise" title="Tortoise">tortoise</a>-like "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastron" title="Plastron">shell shape</a>" used for many depictions of the "zero" numeral, it is assumed not to have influenced <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> numeral systems.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Quipu" title="Quipu">Quipu</a>, a knotted cord device, used in the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> and its predecessor societies in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andean</a> region to record accounting and other digital data, is encoded in a <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">base ten</a> positional system. Zero is represented by the absence of a knot in the appropriate position.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity">Classical antiquity</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a> had no symbol for zero (μηδέν), and did not use a digit placeholder for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> According to mathematician <a href="/wiki/Charles_Seife" title="Charles Seife">Charles Seife</a>, the ancient Greeks did begin to adopt the Babylonian placeholder zero for their work in astronomy after 500 BC, representing it with the lowercase Greek letter <i>ό</i> (<i>όμικρον</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Omicron" title="Omicron">omicron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seife2000_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seife2000-15">[14]</a></sup> However, after using the Babylonian placeholder zero for astronomical calculations they would typically convert the numbers back into <a href="/wiki/Greek_numerals" title="Greek numerals">Greek numerals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seife2000_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seife2000-15">[14]</a></sup> Greeks seemed to have a philosophical opposition to using zero as a number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seife2000_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Seife2000-15">[14]</a></sup>  Other scholars give the Greek partial adoption of the Babylonian zero a later date, with the scientist Andreas Nieder giving a date of after 400 BC and the mathematician Robert Kaplan dating it after the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">conquests of Alexander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nieder2019_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nieder2019-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan1999_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan1999-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Greeks seemed unsure about the status of zero as a number. Some of them asked themselves, "How can not being be?", leading to philosophical and, by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> period, religious arguments about the nature and existence of zero and the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Zeno%27s_paradoxes" title="Zeno's paradoxes">paradoxes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Elea" title="Zeno of Elea">Zeno of Elea</a> depend in large part on the uncertain interpretation of zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P._Lund,_Inv._35a.jpg"><img alt="Fragment of papyrus with clear Greek script, lower-right corner suggests a tiny zero with a double-headed arrow shape above it" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg/300px-P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg/450px-P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/P._Lund%2C_Inv._35a.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P._Lund,_Inv._35a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of the early Greek symbol for zero (lower right corner) from a 2nd-century papyrus</div></div></div>
<p>By AD 150, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a>, was using a symbol for zero (<span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:0.8em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">—</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">°</span></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mercier_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mercier-20">[19]</a></sup> in his work on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_astronomy" title="Mathematical astronomy">mathematical astronomy</a> called the <i>Syntaxis Mathematica</i>, also known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ptolemy_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ptolemy-21">[20]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Greek_numerals#Hellenistic_zero" title="Greek numerals">Hellenistic zero</a> was perhaps the earliest documented use of a numeral representing zero in the Old World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Ptolemy used it many times in his <i>Almagest</i> (VI.8) for the magnitude of <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipses</a>. It represented the value of both <a href="/wiki/Digit_(unit)" title="Digit (unit)">digits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minute_and_second_of_arc" title="Minute and second of arc">minutes</a> of immersion at first and last contact. Digits varied <a href="/wiki/Continuous_function" title="Continuous function">continuously</a> from <span class="nowrap">0 to 12 to 0</span> as the Moon passed over the Sun (a triangular pulse), where twelve digits was the <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular diameter</a> of the Sun. Minutes of immersion was tabulated from <span class="nowrap">0<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.15em;">′</span>0″ to 31<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.15em;">′</span>20″ to 0<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.15em;">′</span>0″</span>, where 0<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.15em;">′</span>0″ used the symbol as a placeholder in two positions of his <a href="/wiki/Sexagesimal" title="Sexagesimal">sexagesimal</a> positional numeral system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[b]</a></sup> while the combination meant a zero angle. Minutes of immersion was also a continuous function <span class="nowrap"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">12</span></span> 31<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.15em;">′</span>20″ <span class="nowrap">√<span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;">d(24−d)</span></span></span> (a triangular pulse with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convex_lens" title="Convex lens">convex</a> sides), where d was the digit function and 31<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.15em;">′</span>20″ was the sum of the radii of the Sun's and Moon's discs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pedersen_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pedersen-24">[22]</a></sup> Ptolemy's symbol was a placeholder as well as a number used by two continuous mathematical functions, one within another, so it meant zero, not none.
</p><p>The earliest use of zero in the calculation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">Julian Easter</a> occurred before AD<span class="nowrap"> </span>311, at the first entry in a table of <a href="/wiki/Epact" title="Epact">epacts</a> as preserved in an <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopic</a> document for the years AD<span class="nowrap"> </span>311 to 369, using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez" title="Ge'ez">Ge'ez</a> word for "none" (English translation is "0" elsewhere) alongside Ge'ez numerals (based on Greek numerals), which was translated from an equivalent table published by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Alexandria" title="Church of Alexandria">Church of Alexandria</a> in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Medieval Greek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neugebauer_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neugebauer-25">[23]</a></sup> This use was repeated in AD<span class="nowrap"> </span>525 in an equivalent table, that was translated via the Latin <i>nulla</i> or "none" by <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_Exiguus" title="Dionysius Exiguus">Dionysius Exiguus</a>, alongside <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals#Zero" title="Roman numerals">Roman numerals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dionysius_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dionysius-26">[24]</a></sup> When division produced zero as a remainder, <i>nihil</i>, meaning "nothing", was used. These medieval zeros were used by all future medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">calculators of Easter</a>. The initial "N" was used as a zero symbol in a table of Roman numerals by <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>—or his colleagues—around AD 725.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zero_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-zero-27">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zero_in_Rod_Calculus.png"><img alt="Five illustrated boxes from left to right contain a T-shape, an empty box, three vertical bars, three lower horizontal bars with an inverted wide T-shape above, and another empty box. Numerals underneath left to right are six, zero, three, nine, and zero" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="293" data-file-width="804" decoding="async" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Zero_in_Rod_Calculus.png/220px-Zero_in_Rod_Calculus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Zero_in_Rod_Calculus.png/330px-Zero_in_Rod_Calculus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Zero_in_Rod_Calculus.png/440px-Zero_in_Rod_Calculus.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zero_in_Rod_Calculus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This is a depiction of zero expressed in Chinese <a href="/wiki/Counting_rods" title="Counting rods">counting rods</a>, based on the example provided by <i>A History of Mathematics</i>. An empty space is used to represent zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodgkin_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgkin-28">[26]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Sunzi_Suanjing" title="Sunzi Suanjing">Sūnzĭ Suànjīng</a></i>, of unknown date but estimated to be dated from the 1st to <span class="nowrap">5th centuries AD</span>, and Japanese records dated from the 18th century, describe how the <span class="nowrap">c. 4th century BC</span> Chinese <a href="/wiki/Counting_rods" title="Counting rods">counting rods</a> system enabled one to perform decimal calculations. As noted in <a href="/wiki/Xiahou_Yang_Suanjing" title="Xiahou Yang Suanjing">Xiahou Yang's Suanjing</a> (425–468 AD) that states that to multiply or divide a number by 10, 100, 1000, or 10000, all one needs to do, with rods on the counting board, is to move them forwards, or back, by 1, 2, 3, or 4 places,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> According to <i>A History of Mathematics</i>, the rods "gave the decimal representation of a number, with an empty space denoting zero".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodgkin_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgkin-28">[26]</a></sup> The counting rod system is considered a <a href="/wiki/Positional_notation" title="Positional notation">positional notation</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>In AD 690, <a href="/wiki/Wu_Zetian" title="Wu Zetian">Empress Wǔ</a> promulgated <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters_of_Empress_Wu" title="Chinese characters of Empress Wu">Zetian characters</a>, one of which was "〇"; originally meaning 'star', it subsequently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2021)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> came to represent zero.
</p><p>Zero was not treated as a number at that time, but as a "vacant position".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crossley_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crossley-31">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Qin_Jiushao" title="Qin Jiushao">Qín Jiǔsháo</a>'s 1247 <i><a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Treatise_in_Nine_Sections" title="Mathematical Treatise in Nine Sections">Mathematical Treatise in Nine Sections</a></i> is the oldest surviving Chinese mathematical text using a round symbol for zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qin_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qin-32">[30]</a></sup> Chinese authors had been familiar with the idea of negative numbers by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> <span class="nowrap">(2nd century AD)</span>, as seen in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nine_Chapters_on_the_Mathematical_Art" title="The Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art">The Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-struik33_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-struik33-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pingala" title="Pingala">Pingala</a> (c. 3rd/2nd century BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plofker_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-plofker-34">[32]</a></sup>), a <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_prosody" title="Sanskrit prosody">Sanskrit prosody</a> scholar,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_numeral_system" title="Binary numeral system">binary numbers</a> in the form of short and long syllables (the latter equal in length to two short syllables), a notation similar to <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">Morse code</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> Pingala used the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C5%ABnyat%C4%81" title="Śūnyatā">śūnya</a></i> explicitly to refer to zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plofker_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-plofker-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of zero as a written digit in the decimal place value notation was developed in <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">India</a>, presumably as early as during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gupta_period" title="Gupta period">Gupta period</a> <span class="nowrap">(c. 5th century)</span>, with the oldest unambiguous evidence dating to the 7th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bourbaki46_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-bourbaki46-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>A symbol for zero, a large dot likely to be the precursor of the still-current hollow symbol, is used throughout the <a href="/wiki/Bakhshali_manuscript" title="Bakhshali manuscript">Bakhshali manuscript</a>, a practical manual on arithmetic for merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-38">[36]</a></sup> In 2017, three samples from the manuscript were shown by <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dating</a> to come from three different centuries: from AD 224–383, AD 680–779, and AD 885–993, making it South Asia's oldest recorded use of the zero symbol. It is not known how the <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a> bark fragments from different centuries forming the manuscript came to be packaged together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Lokavibhaga" title="Lokavibhaga">Lokavibhāga</a></i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jain</a> text on <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a> surviving in a medieval Sanskrit translation of the <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a> original, which is internally dated to AD 458 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saka_era" title="Saka era">Saka era</a> 380), uses a decimal <a href="/wiki/Positional_notation" title="Positional notation">place-value system</a>, including a zero. In this text, <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C5%ABnyat%C4%81" title="Śūnyatā">śūnya</a></i> ("void, empty") is also used to refer to zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Aryabhatiya" title="Aryabhatiya">Aryabhatiya</a></i> (c. 500), states <i>sthānāt sthānaṁ daśaguṇaṁ syāt</i> "from place to place each is ten times the preceding".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aryab_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aryab-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rules governing the use of zero appeared in <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Br%C4%81hmasphu%E1%B9%ADasiddh%C4%81nta" title="Brāhmasphuṭasiddhānta">Brahmasputha Siddhanta</a></i> (7th century), which states the sum of zero with itself as zero, and incorrectly <a href="/wiki/Division_by_zero" title="Division by zero">division by zero</a> as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brahmagupta_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-brahmagupta-46">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>A positive or negative number when divided by zero is a fraction with the zero as denominator. Zero divided by a negative or positive number is either zero or is expressed as a fraction with zero as numerator and the finite quantity as denominator. Zero divided by zero is zero.</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Epigraphy">Epigraphy</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khmer_Numerals_-_605_from_the_Sambor_inscriptions.jpg"><img alt="script from left to right with a one and a half rotation swirl, a large dot, and a stretched-bent swirl" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="157" data-file-width="244" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Khmer_Numerals_-_605_from_the_Sambor_inscriptions.jpg/220px-Khmer_Numerals_-_605_from_the_Sambor_inscriptions.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Khmer_Numerals_-_605_from_the_Sambor_inscriptions.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khmer_Numerals_-_605_from_the_Sambor_inscriptions.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The number 605 in Khmer numerals, from the Sambor inscription (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saka_era" title="Saka era">Saka era</a> 605 corresponds to AD 683). The earliest known material use of zero as a decimal figure.</div></div></div>
<p>A black dot is used as a decimal placeholder in the <a href="/wiki/Bakhshali_manuscript" title="Bakhshali manuscript">Bakhshali manuscript</a>, portions of which date from AD 224–383.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are numerous copper plate inscriptions, with the same small <i>o</i> in them, some of them possibly dated to the 6th century, but their date or authenticity may be open to doubt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiref1_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-multiref1-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>A stone tablet found in the ruins of a temple near Sambor on the <a href="/wiki/Mekong" title="Mekong">Mekong</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krati%C3%A9_Province" title="Kratié Province">Kratié Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, includes the inscription of "605" in <a href="/wiki/Khmer_numerals" title="Khmer numerals">Khmer numerals</a> (a set of numeral glyphs for the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a>). The number is the year of the inscription in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saka_era" title="Saka era">Saka era</a>, corresponding to a date of AD 683.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambodia_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambodia-49">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first known use of special <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">glyphs</a> for the decimal digits that includes the indubitable appearance of a symbol for the digit zero, a small circle, appears on a stone inscription found at the <a href="/wiki/Chaturbhuj_Temple,_Gwalior" title="Chaturbhuj Temple, Gwalior">Chaturbhuj Temple, Gwalior</a>, in India, dated 876.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission_to_Islamic_culture">Transmission to Islamic culture</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="History of the Hindu–Arabic numeral system">History of the Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>-language inheritance of science was largely <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greek</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> followed by Hindu influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durant_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Durant-53">[51]</a></sup> In 773, at <a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur" title="Al-Mansur">Al-Mansur</a>'s behest, translations were made of many ancient treatises including Greek, Roman, Indian, and others.
</p><p>In AD 813, astronomical tables were prepared by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> mathematician, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%E1%B8%A5ammad_ibn_M%C5%ABs%C4%81_al-Khw%C4%81rizm%C4%AB" title="Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī">Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī</a>, using Hindu numerals;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durant_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Durant-53">[51]</a></sup> and about 825, he published a book synthesizing Greek and Hindu knowledge and also contained his own contribution to mathematics including an explanation of the use of zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> This book was later translated into <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> in the 12th century under the title <i>Algoritmi de numero Indorum</i>. This title means "al-Khwarizmi on the Numerals of the Indians". The word "Algoritmi" was the translator's Latinization of Al-Khwarizmi's name, and the word "Algorithm" or "Algorism" started to acquire a meaning of any arithmetic based on decimals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durant_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Durant-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Ahmad_al-Khwarizmi" title="Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Khwarizmi">Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Khwarizmi</a>, in 976, stated that if no number appears in the place of tens in a calculation, a little circle should be used "to keep the rows". This circle was called <i>ṣifr</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission_to_Europe">Transmission to Europe</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a> (base 10) reached Western Europe in the 11th century, via <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>, through Spanish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>, together with knowledge of classical  <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and instruments like the <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabe</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sylvester_II" title="Pope Sylvester II">Gerbert of Aurillac</a> is credited with reintroducing the lost teachings into Catholic Europe. For this reason, the numerals came to be known in Europe as "Arabic numerals". The Italian mathematician <a href="/wiki/Fibonacci" title="Fibonacci">Fibonacci</a> or Leonardo of Pisa was instrumental in bringing the system into European mathematics in 1202, stating:
</p>
<blockquote><p>After my father's appointment by his homeland as state official in the customs house of Bugia for the Pisan merchants who thronged to it, he took charge; and in view of its future usefulness and convenience, had me in my boyhood come to him and there wanted me to devote myself to and be instructed in the study of calculation for some days. There, following my introduction, as a consequence of marvelous instruction in the art, to the nine digits of the Hindus, the knowledge of the art very much appealed to me before all others, and for it I realized that all its aspects were studied in Egypt, Syria, Greece, Sicily, and Provence, with their varying methods; and at these places thereafter, while on business. I pursued my study in depth and learned the give-and-take of disputation. But all this even, and the algorism, as well as the art of Pythagoras, I considered as almost a mistake in respect to the method of the <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindus</a> (Modus Indorum). Therefore, embracing more stringently that method of the Hindus, and taking stricter pains in its study, while adding certain things from my own understanding and inserting also certain things from the niceties of Euclid's geometric art. I have striven to compose this book in its entirety as understandably as I could, dividing it into fifteen chapters. Almost everything which I have introduced I have displayed with exact proof, in order that those further seeking this knowledge, with its pre-eminent method, might be instructed, and further, in order that the Latin people might not be discovered to be without it, as they have been up to now. If I have perchance omitted anything more or less proper or necessary, I beg indulgence, since there is no one who is blameless and utterly provident in all things. The nine Indian figures are: 9 8 7 6 5 4 3 2 1. With these nine figures, and with the sign 0  ... any number may be written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Here Leonardo of Pisa uses the phrase "sign 0", indicating it is like a sign to do operations like addition or multiplication. From the 13th century, manuals on calculation (adding, multiplying, extracting roots, etc.) became common in Europe where they were called <i><a href="/wiki/Algorism" title="Algorism">algorismus</a></i> after the Persian mathematician al-Khwārizmī. The most popular was written by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_de_Sacrobosco" title="Johannes de Sacrobosco">Johannes de Sacrobosco</a>, about 1235 and was one of the earliest scientific books to be <i>printed</i> in 1488. Until the late 15th century, Hindu–Arabic numerals seem to have predominated among mathematicians, while merchants preferred to use the <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">Roman numerals</a>. In the 16th century, they became commonly used in Europe.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">Mathematics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Null_(mathematics)" title="Null (mathematics)">Null (mathematics)</a></div>
<p>0 is the <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integer</a> immediately preceding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_(number)" title="1 (number)">1</a>. <a href="/wiki/Parity_of_zero" title="Parity of zero">Zero is an even number</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> because it is divisible by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2_(number)" title="2 (number)">2</a> with no remainder. 0 is neither positive nor negative,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> or both positive and negative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> Many definitions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> include 0 as a <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural number</a>, in which case it is the only natural number that is not positive. Zero is a number which quantifies a count or an amount of <a href="/wiki/Empty_set" title="Empty set">null</a> size. In most <a href="/wiki/History_of_mathematics" title="History of mathematics">cultures</a>, 0 was identified before the idea of negative things (i.e., quantities less than zero) was accepted.
</p><p>As a value or a <i>number</i>, zero is not the same as the <i>digit</i> zero, used in <a href="/wiki/Numeral_system" title="Numeral system">numeral systems</a> with <a href="/wiki/Positional_notation" title="Positional notation">positional notation</a>. Successive positions of digits have higher weights, so the digit zero is used inside a numeral to skip a position and give appropriate weights to the preceding and following digits. A zero digit is not always necessary in a positional number system (e.g., the number 02). In some instances, a <a href="/wiki/Leading_zero" title="Leading zero">leading zero</a> may be used to distinguish a number.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elementary_algebra">Elementary algebra</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Number_line_with_numbers_-3_to_3.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="54" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="35" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Number_line_with_numbers_-3_to_3.svg/300px-Number_line_with_numbers_-3_to_3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Number_line_with_numbers_-3_to_3.svg/450px-Number_line_with_numbers_-3_to_3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Number_line_with_numbers_-3_to_3.svg/600px-Number_line_with_numbers_-3_to_3.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Number_line_with_numbers_-3_to_3.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Number_line" title="Number line">number line</a> from -3 to 3, with 0 in the middle</div></div></div>
<p>The number 0 is the smallest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-negative" title="Non-negative">non-negative</a> integer. The <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural number</a> following 0 is 1 and no natural number precedes 0. The number 0 <a href="/wiki/Natural_number#History" title="Natural number">may or may not be considered a natural number</a>, but it is an integer, and hence a <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational number</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real number</a> (as well as an <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number" title="Algebraic number">algebraic number</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex number</a>).
</p><p>The number 0 is neither positive nor negative, and is usually displayed as the central number in a <a href="/wiki/Number_line" title="Number line">number line</a>. It is neither a <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime number</a> nor a <a href="/wiki/Composite_number" title="Composite number">composite number</a>. It cannot be prime because it has an <a href="/wiki/Infinity" title="Infinity">infinite</a> number of <a href="/wiki/Divisor" title="Divisor">factors</a>, and cannot be composite because it cannot be expressed as a product of prime numbers (as 0 must always be one of the factors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> Zero is, however, <a href="/wiki/Parity_(mathematics)" title="Parity (mathematics)">even</a> (i.e. a multiple of 2, as well as being a multiple of any other integer, rational, or real number).
</p><p>The following are some basic (elementary) rules for dealing with the number 0. These rules apply for any real or complex number <i>x</i>, unless otherwise stated.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Addition" title="Addition">Addition</a>: <i>x</i> + 0 = 0 + <i>x</i> = <i>x</i>. That is, 0 is an <a href="/wiki/Identity_element" title="Identity element">identity element</a> (or neutral element) with respect to addition.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Subtraction" title="Subtraction">Subtraction</a>: <i>x</i> − 0 = <i>x</i> and 0 − <i>x</i> = −<i>x</i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Multiplication" title="Multiplication">Multiplication</a>: <i>x</i> · 0 = 0 · <i>x</i> = 0.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_(mathematics)" title="Division (mathematics)">Division</a>: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">0</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>x</i></span></span> = 0, for nonzero <i>x</i>. But <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>x</i></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">0</span></span> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Defined_and_undefined" title="Defined and undefined">undefined</a>, because 0 has no <a href="/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse" title="Multiplicative inverse">multiplicative inverse</a> (no real number multiplied by 0 produces 1), a consequence of the previous rule.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Exponentiation" title="Exponentiation">Exponentiation</a>: <i>x</i><sup>0</sup> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>x</i></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>x</i></span></span> = 1, except that the case <i>x</i> = 0 may be left undefined in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/0_to_the_power_of_0" title="0 to the power of 0">contexts</a>. For all positive real <i>x</i>, <span class="nowrap">0<sup><i>x</i></sup> = 0</span>.</li></ul>
<p>The expression <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">0</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">0</span></span>, which may be obtained in an attempt to determine the limit of an expression of the form <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>)</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>g</i>(<i>x</i>)</span></span> as a result of applying the <a href="/wiki/Limit_of_a_function" title="Limit of a function">lim</a> operator independently to both operands of the fraction, is a so-called "<a href="/wiki/Indeterminate_form" title="Indeterminate form">indeterminate form</a>". That does not simply mean that the limit sought is necessarily undefined; rather, it means that the limit of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>)</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>g</i>(<i>x</i>)</span></span>, if it exists, must be found by another method, such as <a href="/wiki/L%27H%C3%B4pital%27s_rule" title="L'Hôpital's rule">l'Hôpital's rule</a>.
</p><p>The sum of 0 numbers (the <i><a href="/wiki/Empty_sum" title="Empty sum">empty sum</a></i>) is 0, and the product of 0 numbers (the <i><a href="/wiki/Empty_product" title="Empty product">empty product</a></i>) is 1. The <a href="/wiki/Factorial" title="Factorial">factorial</a> 0! evaluates to 1, as a special case of the empty product.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_branches_of_mathematics">Other branches of mathematics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nullset.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="163" data-file-width="126" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Nullset.svg/100px-Nullset.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Nullset.svg/150px-Nullset.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Nullset.svg/200px-Nullset.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nullset.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The empty set has zero elements</div></div></div>
<ul><li>In <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a>, 0 is the <a href="/wiki/Cardinality" title="Cardinality">cardinality</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Empty_set" title="Empty set">empty set</a>: if one does not have any apples, then one has 0 apples. In fact, in certain axiomatic developments of mathematics from set theory, 0 is <i><a href="/wiki/Definition" title="Definition">defined</a></i> to be the empty set. When this is done, the empty set is the <a href="/wiki/Von_Neumann_cardinal_assignment" title="Von Neumann cardinal assignment">von Neumann cardinal assignment</a> for a set with no elements, which is the empty set. The cardinality function, applied to the empty set, returns the empty set as a value, thereby assigning it 0 elements.</li>
<li>Also in set theory, 0 is the lowest <a href="/wiki/Ordinal_number" title="Ordinal number">ordinal number</a>, corresponding to the empty set viewed as a <a href="/wiki/Well-order" title="Well-order">well-ordered set</a>.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Propositional_calculus" title="Propositional calculus">propositional logic</a>, 0 may be used to denote the <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">truth value</a> false.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a>, 0 is commonly used to denote a <a href="/wiki/Zero_element" title="Zero element">zero element</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Identity_element" title="Identity element">neutral element</a> for addition (if defined on the structure under consideration) and an <a href="/wiki/Absorbing_element" title="Absorbing element">absorbing element</a> for multiplication (if defined).</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Lattice_(order)" title="Lattice (order)">lattice theory</a>, 0 may denote the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greatest_element" title="Greatest element">bottom element</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Lattice_(order)" title="Lattice (order)">bounded lattice</a>.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Category_theory" title="Category theory">category theory</a>, 0 is sometimes used to denote an <a href="/wiki/Initial_and_terminal_objects" title="Initial and terminal objects">initial object</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Category_(mathematics)" title="Category (mathematics)">category</a>.</li>
<li>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recursion_theory" title="Recursion theory">recursion theory</a>, 0 can be used to denote the <a href="/wiki/Turing_degree" title="Turing degree">Turing degree</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Computable_function" title="Computable function">partial computable functions</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_mathematical_terms">Related mathematical terms</span></h3>
<ul><li>A <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_of_a_function" title="Root of a function">zero of a function</a></i> <i>f</i> is a point <i>x</i> in the domain of the function such that <span class="nowrap"><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) = 0</span>. When there are finitely many zeros these are called the roots of the function. This is related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero_(complex_analysis)" title="Zero (complex analysis)">zeros</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Holomorphic_function" title="Holomorphic function">holomorphic function</a>.</li>
<li>The <b>zero function</b> (or <b>zero map</b>) on a domain <i>D</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Constant_function" title="Constant function">constant function</a> with 0 as its only possible output value, i.e., the function <i>f</i> defined by <span class="nowrap"><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) = 0</span> for all <i>x</i> in <i>D</i>. The zero function is the only function that is both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Even_function" title="Even function">even</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odd_function" title="Odd function">odd</a>. A particular zero function is a <a href="/wiki/Zero_morphism" title="Zero morphism">zero morphism</a> in category theory; e.g., a zero map is the identity in the additive group of functions. The <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">determinant</a> on non-invertible <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">square matrices</a> is a zero map.</li>
<li>Several branches of mathematics have <a href="/wiki/Zero_element" title="Zero element">zero elements</a>, which generalize either the property <span class="nowrap">0 + <i>x</i> = <i>x</i></span>, or the property <span class="nowrap">0 · <i>x</i> = 0,</span> or both.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span></h2>
<p>The value zero plays a special role for many physical quantities. For some quantities, the zero level is naturally distinguished from all other levels, whereas for others it is more or less arbitrarily chosen. For example, for an <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">absolute temperature</a> (as measured in <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a>), <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">zero</a> is the lowest possible value (<a href="/wiki/Negative_temperature" title="Negative temperature">negative temperatures</a> are defined, but negative-temperature systems are not actually colder). This is in contrast to for example temperatures on the Celsius scale, where zero is arbitrarily defined to be at the <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">freezing point</a> of water. Measuring sound intensity in <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">decibels</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phon" title="Phon">phons</a>, the zero level is arbitrarily set at a reference value—for example, at a value for the threshold of hearing. In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Zero-point_energy" title="Zero-point energy">zero-point energy</a> is the lowest possible energy that a <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> <a href="/wiki/Physical_system" title="Physical system">physical system</a> may possess and is the energy of the <a href="/wiki/Stationary_state" title="Stationary state">ground state</a> of the system.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h2>
<p>Zero has been proposed as the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> of the theoretical element <a href="/wiki/Tetraneutron" title="Tetraneutron">tetraneutron</a>. It has been shown that a cluster of four <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> may be stable enough to be considered an <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> in its own right. This would create an <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> with no <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and no charge on its <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a>.
</p><p>As early as 1926, Andreas von Antropoff coined the term <a href="/wiki/Neutronium" title="Neutronium">neutronium</a> for a conjectured form of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> made up of neutrons with no protons, which he placed as the chemical element of atomic number zero at the head of his new version of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. It was subsequently placed as a noble gas in the middle of several spiral representations of the periodic system for classifying the chemical elements.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_science">Computer science</span></h2>
<p>The most common practice throughout human history has been to start counting at one, and this is the practice in early classic <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">computer programming</a> languages such as <a href="/wiki/Fortran" title="Fortran">Fortran</a> and <a href="/wiki/COBOL" title="COBOL">COBOL</a>. However, in the late 1950s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LISP" title="LISP">LISP</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Zero-based_numbering" title="Zero-based numbering">zero-based numbering</a> for arrays while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algol_58" title="Algol 58">Algol 58</a> introduced completely flexible basing for array subscripts (allowing any positive, negative, or zero integer as base for array subscripts), and most subsequent programming languages adopted one or other of these positions. For example, the elements of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Array_data_type" title="Array data type">array</a> are numbered starting from 0 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C_(computer_language)" title="C (computer language)">C</a>, so that for an array of <i>n</i> items the sequence of array indices runs from 0 to <span class="nowrap"><i>n</i>−1</span>. This permits an array element's location to be calculated by adding the index directly to address of the array, whereas 1-based languages precalculate the array's base address to be the position one element before the first.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>There can be confusion between 0- and 1-based indexing; for example, Java's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JDBC" title="JDBC">JDBC</a> indexes parameters from 1 although <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> itself uses 0-based indexing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In databases, it is possible for a field not to have a value. It is then said to have a <a href="/wiki/Null_(SQL)" title="Null (SQL)">null value</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> For numeric fields it is not the value zero. For text fields this is not blank nor the empty string. The presence of null values leads to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ternary_logic" title="Ternary logic">three-valued logic</a>. No longer is a condition either <i>true</i> or <i>false</i>, but it can be <i>undetermined</i>. Any computation including a null value delivers a null result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Null_pointer" title="Null pointer">null pointer</a> is a pointer in a computer program that does not point to any object or function. In C, the integer constant 0 is converted into the null pointer at <a href="/wiki/Compile_time" title="Compile time">compile time</a> when it appears in a pointer context, and so 0 is a standard way to refer to the null pointer in code. However, the internal representation of the null pointer may be any bit pattern (possibly different values for different data types).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In mathematics <span class="nowrap">−0 = +0 = 0;</span> both −0 and +0 represent exactly the same number, i.e., there is no "positive zero" or "negative zero" distinct from zero. However, in some computer hardware <a href="/wiki/Signed_number_representations" title="Signed number representations">signed number representations</a>, zero has two distinct representations, a positive one grouped with the positive numbers and a negative one grouped with the negatives; this kind of dual representation is known as <a href="/wiki/Signed_zero" title="Signed zero">signed zero</a>, with the latter form sometimes called negative zero. These representations include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signed_magnitude" title="Signed magnitude">signed magnitude</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One%27s_complement" title="One's complement">one's complement</a> binary integer representations (but not the <a href="/wiki/Two%27s_complement" title="Two's complement">two's complement</a> binary form used in most modern computers), and most <a href="/wiki/Floating-point_arithmetic" title="Floating-point arithmetic">floating-point</a> number representations (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE_floating_point" title="IEEE floating point">IEEE 754</a> and <a href="/wiki/IBM_hexadecimal_floating-point" title="IBM hexadecimal floating-point">IBM S/390</a> floating-point formats).
</p><p>In binary, 0 represents the value for "off", which means no electricity flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChris_Woodford20069_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChris_Woodford20069-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zero is the value of false in many programming languages.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unix_epoch" title="Unix epoch">Unix epoch</a> (the date and time associated with a zero timestamp) begins the midnight before the first of January 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Classic_Mac_OS" title="Classic Mac OS">Classic Mac OS</a> <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(computing)" title="Epoch (computing)">epoch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palm_OS" title="Palm OS">Palm OS</a> epoch (the date and time associated with a zero timestamp) begins the midnight before the first of January 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">APIs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a> that require applications to return an integer value as an <a href="/wiki/Exit_status" title="Exit status">exit status</a> typically use zero to indicate success and non-zero values to indicate specific <a href="/wiki/Error_code" title="Error code">error</a> or warning conditions.
</p><p>Programmers often use a <a href="/wiki/Slashed_zero#Usage" title="Slashed zero">slashed zero</a> to avoid confusion with the letter "<a href="/wiki/O" title="O">O</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_fields">Other fields</span></h2>
<ul><li>In <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">comparative zoology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>, recognition that some animals display awareness of the concept of zero leads to the conclusion that the capability for numerical abstraction arose early in the evolution of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup></li>
<li>In telephony, pressing 0 is often used for dialling out of a <a href="/wiki/Business_telephone_system" title="Business telephone system">company network</a> or to a different <a href="/wiki/Trunk_prefix" title="Trunk prefix">city or region</a>, and 00 is used for dialling <a href="/wiki/International_call_prefix" title="International call prefix">abroad</a>. In some countries, dialling 0 places a call for <a href="/wiki/Operator_assistance" title="Operator assistance">operator assistance</a>.</li>
<li>DVDs that can be played in any region are sometimes referred to as being "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Region_0" title="Region 0">region 0</a>"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Roulette" title="Roulette">Roulette</a> wheels usually feature a "0" space (and sometimes also a "00" space), whose presence is ignored when calculating payoffs (thereby allowing the house to win in the long run).</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a>, if the reigning <a href="/wiki/List_of_Formula_One_World_Drivers%27_Champions" title="List of Formula One World Drivers' Champions">World Champion</a> no longer competes in Formula One in the year following their victory in the title race, 0 is given to one of the drivers of the team that the reigning champion won the title with. This happened in 1993 and 1994, with <a href="/wiki/Damon_Hill" title="Damon Hill">Damon Hill</a> driving car 0, due to the reigning World Champion (<a href="/wiki/Nigel_Mansell" title="Nigel Mansell">Nigel Mansell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alain_Prost" title="Alain Prost">Alain Prost</a> respectively) not competing in the championship.</li>
<li>On the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate Highway System</a>, in most states exits are numbered based on the nearest milepost from the highway's western or southern terminus within that state. Several that are less than half a mile (800 m) from state boundaries in that direction are numbered as Exit 0.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols_and_representations">Symbols and representations</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Symbols_for_zero" title="Symbols for zero">Symbols for zero</a></div>
<div class="floatleft"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Text_figures_036.svg"><img alt="horizontal guidelines with a zero touching top and bottom, a three dipping below, and a six cresting above the guidelines, from left to right" data-file-height="41" data-file-width="85" decoding="async" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Text_figures_036.svg/71px-Text_figures_036.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Text_figures_036.svg/107px-Text_figures_036.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Text_figures_036.svg/142px-Text_figures_036.svg.png 2x" width="71"/></a></div>
<p>The modern numerical digit 0 is usually written as a circle or ellipse. Traditionally, many print typefaces made the capital letter <a href="/wiki/O" title="O">O</a> more rounded than the narrower, elliptical digit 0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bemer_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-bemer-74">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">Typewriters</a> originally made no distinction in shape between O and 0; some models did not even have a separate key for the digit 0. The distinction came into prominence on modern character <a href="/wiki/Visual_display_unit" title="Visual display unit">displays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bemer_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-bemer-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Slashed_zero" title="Slashed zero">slashed zero</a> (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 0\!\!\!{/}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>0</mn>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 0\!\!\!{/}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle 0\!\!\!{/}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4059f8b4ddbe8f89a13eb4ccc1e87cf3e776d8db" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.164ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>) can be used to distinguish the number from the letter (mostly used in computing, navigation and in the military). The digit 0 with a dot in the center seems to have originated as an option on <a href="/wiki/IBM_3270" title="IBM 3270">IBM 3270</a> displays and has continued with some modern computer typefaces such as <a href="/wiki/Andal%C3%A9_Mono" title="Andalé Mono">Andalé Mono</a>, and in some airline reservation systems. One variation uses a short vertical bar instead of the dot. Some fonts designed for use with computers made one of the capital-O–digit-0 pair more rounded and the other more angular (closer to a rectangle). A further distinction is made in <a href="/wiki/FE-Schrift" title="FE-Schrift">falsification-hindering typeface</a> as used on <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plates_of_Germany" title="Vehicle registration plates of Germany">German car number plates</a> by slitting open the digit 0 on the upper right side. In some systems either the letter O or the numeral 0, or both, are excluded from use, to avoid confusion.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Year_label">Year label</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Year_zero" title="Year zero">Year zero</a></div>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_Christ" title="Before Christ">BC</a> <a href="/wiki/Calendar_era" title="Calendar era">calendar era</a>, the year 1 BC is the first year before AD 1; there is not a <a href="/wiki/Year_zero" title="Year zero">year zero</a>. By contrast, in <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_year_numbering" title="Astronomical year numbering">astronomical year numbering</a>, the year 1 BC is numbered 0, the year 2 BC is numbered −1, and so forth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">No long count date actually using the number 0 has been found before the 3rd century AD, but since the long count system would make no sense without some placeholder, and since Mesoamerican glyphs do not typically leave empty spaces, these earlier dates are taken as indirect evidence that the concept of 0 already existed at the time.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Each place in Ptolemy's sexagesimal system was written in <a href="/wiki/Greek_numerals" title="Greek numerals">Greek numerals</a> from <span class="nowrap">0 to 59</span>, where 31 was written λα meaning 30+1, and 20 was written κ meaning 20.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-plofker-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-plofker_34-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-plofker_34-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPlofker2009"><a href="/wiki/Kim_Plofker" title="Kim Plofker">Plofker, Kim</a> (2009). <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_India_(book)" title="Mathematics in India (book)"><i>Mathematics in India</i></a>. Princeton University Press. pp. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=DHvThPNp9yMC&amp;pg=PA54" rel="nofollow">54–56</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-691-12067-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-691-12067-6"><bdi>978-0-691-12067-6</bdi></a>. <q>In the Chandah-sutra of Pingala, dating perhaps the third or second century BC, [ ...] Pingala's use of a zero symbol [śūnya] as a marker seems to be the first known explicit reference to zero. ... In the Chandah-sutra of Pingala, dating perhaps the third or second century BC, there are five questions concerning the possible meters for any value "n". [ ...] The answer is (2)<sup>7</sup> = 128, as expected, but instead of seven doublings, the process (explained by the sutra) required only three doublings and two squarings – a handy time saver where "n" is large. Pingala's use of a zero symbol as a marker seems to be the first known explicit reference to zero</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Mathematics+in+India&amp;rft.pages=54-56&amp;rft.pub=Princeton+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-691-12067-6&amp;rft.aulast=Plofker&amp;rft.aufirst=Kim&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3A0"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPannekoek1961">Pannekoek, A. (1961). <i>A History of Astronomy</i>. George Allen &amp; Unwin. p. 165.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+Astronomy&amp;rft.pages=165&amp;rft.pub=George+Allen+%26+Unwin&amp;rft.date=1961&amp;rft.aulast=Pannekoek&amp;rft.aufirst=A.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3A0"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Durant-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Durant_53-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Durant_53-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Durant_53-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Will Durant (1950), <i>The Story of Civilization</i>, Volume 4, The Age of Faith: Constantine to Dante – A.D. 325–1300, Simon &amp; Schuster, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-9650007-5-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-9650007-5-8">978-0-9650007-5-8</a>, p. 241, "The Arabic inheritance of science was overwhelmingly Greek, but Hindu influences ranked next. In 773, at Mansur's behest, translations were made of the <i>Siddhantas</i> – Indian astronomical treatises dating as far back as 425 BC; these versions may have the vehicle through which the "Arabic" numerals and the zero were brought from India into Islam. In 813, al-Khwarizmi used the Hindu numerals in his astronomical tables."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFBrezina2006">Brezina, Corona (2006). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=955jPgAACAAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Al-Khwarizmi: The Inventor of Algebra</i></a>. The Rosen Publishing Group. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4042-0513-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4042-0513-0"><bdi>978-1-4042-0513-0</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200229035314/https://books.google.com/books?id=955jPgAACAAJ" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 29 February 2020<span class="reference-accessdate">. </li>
<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Will Durant (1950), <i>The Story of Civilization</i>, Volume 4, The Age of Faith, Simon &amp; Schuster, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-9650007-5-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-9650007-5-8">978-0-9650007-5-8</a>, p. 241, "In 976, Muhammad ibn Ahmad, in his <i>Keys of the Sciences</i>, remarked that if, in a calculation, no number appears in the place of tens, a little circle should be used "to keep the rows". This circle the Mosloems called <i>ṣifr</i>, "empty" whence our cipher."</span>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">23 November</span> 2021</span>. <q>In regard to services, sending a null value as an argument in a remote service call means that no data is sent.
